## Activity 1: Analyzing Apicius Recipes - Reconstructing Roman Flavors

**Objective:** Students analyze actual Apicius recipes to understand Roman flavor preferences, identify unfamiliar ingredients, consider cultural differences in taste, and reflect on how culinary norms shift across time and culture.

**Instructions:**

Below are three authentic recipes from Apicius's *De Re Coquinaria* (translated into English with slight modernization for clarity). Read each recipe carefully, then answer the questions that follow.

**RECIPE A: Duck with Turnips** (Book 6, Recipe 231)
*Clean and prepare duck. Put in pot with water, salt, dill, and vinegar. Parboil. Then add bundle of leeks and coriander. When nearly done, add chopped turnips to cook. Make sauce: pound pepper, cumin, coriander seed, laser root [similar to asafoetida], mint, rue. Mix with vinegar, honey, garum, and cooking liquid. Pour over duck. Sprinkle with pepper and serve.*

**RECIPE B: Sweet Wine Cakes** (Book 7, Recipe 121)
*Break fine white bread, soak in milk. Fry in oil. Pour honey over. Sprinkle with pepper and serve.*

**RECIPE C: Minute Stew for Sick Persons** (Book 4, Recipe 182)
*Cook lentils with leeks and chestnuts. Pound pepper, cumin, coriander seed, mint, rue, pennyroyal. Moisten with vinegar. Add dates and honey. Mix with cooking liquid and garum and wine. Pour back over lentils. Add oil. Serve.*

**Questions:**

1) **Identify unfamiliar patterns:** Choose one recipe. What ingredient combination surprises you or seems unusual compared to modern cooking? Explain specifically what's unexpected.

2) **The sweet-savory fusion:** All three recipes contain elements modern cooks might consider contradictory. Identify two examples of sweet ingredients paired with savory/salty ingredients. Why do you think Romans enjoyed these combinations?

3) **Garum's cultural role:** Notice *garum* appears even in the sick person's stew (Recipe C). What does *garum*'s presence in medicinal food suggest about how Romans viewed it? Compare to a modern ingredient used similarly.

4) **Recipe B analysis:** The sweet wine cake (Recipe B) ends with "sprinkle with pepper." This is DESSERT topped with BLACK PEPPER. Most modern desserts avoid black pepper entirely. Propose two possible explanations for why Romans used pepper on sweet dishes.

5) **Attempting translation:** Choose Recipe A or C. Rewrite the recipe in modern format (list ingredients with measurements on left, numbered instructions on right) as you would find in contemporary cookbook. Where do you encounter problems? What information is missing?

6) **Timothy's challenge:** Imagine Timothy wants to recreate Recipe A for his culinary class, but needs to substitute *laser* (extinct plant) and *rue* (rarely available). Research and suggest two modern ingredients that might approximate the original's pungent, slightly bitter flavor profile.

7) **Class reflection:** How do these recipes challenge the statement "Italian food is based on ancient Roman cooking"? What seems continuous? What seems radically different?

---

## Activity 1: Answer Key with Explanations

**1) Identify unfamiliar patterns:**

*Model Answer:* In Recipe A (Duck with Turnips), the sauce combining honey, vinegar, *garum* (fish sauce), and spices including pepper, cumin, coriander, mint, and rue is surprising. Modern duck preparations rarely combine fish sauce with honey and vinegar—we tend to separate Asian-inspired soy/fish sauce dishes from European honey-glazed roasts. The Romans' willingness to layer sweet (honey), sour (vinegar), salty-umami (*garum*), and multiple complex spices in one sauce creates flavor profile modern Western cooking usually avoids as "too busy" or conflicting.

*Alternative answers (Recipe B):* Pepper on honey-soaked dessert is extremely unusual to modern tastes. We've strictly separated sweet desserts from savory spices.

*Alternative (Recipe C):* Dates (very sweet dried fruit) combined with *garum* (extremely savory fermented fish sauce) in stew for sick people seems bizarre—modern "sick food" like chicken soup avoids sweet elements entirely, focusing on savory comfort.

**Grading notes:** Accept any recipe with specific ingredient identification and clear explanation of why it's unusual to modern sensibilities. Students should demonstrate they recognize the sweet-savory-spicy layering as culturally specific to Romans, not universal.

**2) The sweet-savory fusion examples:**

*Model Answer—Two examples:*

- **Recipe A**: Honey (sweet) combined with *garum* (salty-savory fish sauce) in the same sauce
- **Recipe C**: Dates and honey (both very sweet) combined with *garum* (savory) and vinegar (sour) in lentil stew

*Why Romans enjoyed these:* Romans apparently valued complexity and intensity of flavor, preferring layered taste experiences where sweet, sour, salty, and spicy components balanced each other rather than remaining separate. This created what they perceived as sophisticated, interesting flavors—palate constantly stimulated by contrasts. Modern Southeast Asian cuisines (Thai, Vietnamese) similarly combine sweet-sour-salty-spicy in single dishes, suggesting this isn't inherently "wrong" but culturally specific. Romans may have also believed these combinations aided digestion or balanced humors according to medical theories.

**Grading notes:** Students should identify two clear sweet-savory pairings and provide thoughtful explanation beyond "they had weird taste." Looking for understanding that flavor preferences are culturally constructed and that complexity/intensity were Roman values.

**3) *Garum*'s cultural role:**

*Model Answer:* *Garum*'s inclusion in food for sick people (Recipe C) suggests Romans viewed it as healthful, nourishing, and comforting—not just flavor enhancer but beneficial substance with medicinal/nutritional properties. It was their baseline "good for you" ingredient assumed to aid recovery. 

*Modern comparison:* Chicken broth/bone broth plays similar cultural role today—we give chicken soup to sick people believing it's nourishing and healing, add broth to recipes for depth and nutrition, and view it as wholesome fundamental ingredient rather than optional flavoring. Just as modern cooks might add broth to almost anything for richness and health benefits, Romans added *garum* ubiquitously. Another comparison: salt—we consider salt essential to proper nutrition (in moderation) and add it to nearly everything including some sweets; Romans viewed *garum* similarly but it provided salt plus protein/umami/amino acids.

**Grading notes:** Accept any answer demonstrating understanding that *garum* was culturally normalized as healthful staple, not exotic oddity. Modern comparisons should be thoughtful (salt, broth, olive oil, soy sauce in East Asian cultures all work).

**4) Recipe B analysis—pepper on dessert:**

*Model answers (any two):*

**Explanation 1—Different pepper variety:** Romans may have used pepper varieties with different flavor profiles than modern black peppercorns—long pepper (*piper longum*) from India has slightly sweeter, more complex flavor that might pair better with honey. Modern black pepper's sharp, piney heat isn't perfectly analogous.

**Explanation 2—Balance and complexity:** Romans valued balanced complexity—pepper's heat and aromatic quality contrasted with honey's pure sweetness, preventing dessert from being cloying. Similar to how modern cooks use salt in caramel or chocolate desserts to heighten other flavors, Romans may have used pepper's bite to accentuate honey's sweetness through contrast.

**Explanation 3—Medicinal beliefs:** Roman medical theory (humoral medicine) believed foods had inherent properties—honey might be classified as "wet" and "warm," while pepper was "hot" and "dry." Combining them could balance humoral properties, making dessert healthier or easier to digest according to their medical framework.

**Explanation 4—Cost and status signaling:** Pepper was expensive imported luxury from India. Sprinkling it even on desserts demonstrated wealth and sophistication—"I can afford to use pepper on EVERYTHING, even sweets where it's unexpected."

**Explanation 5—Cultural norm:** What tastes "wrong" to us was normal to Romans who grew up with these combinations. Like how some modern cultures combine chili with chocolate (Mexican *mole*, spicy hot chocolate) while others find it strange, Roman palates were acculturated to pepper-honey pairings.

**Grading notes:** Accept any two reasonable explanations showing critical thinking. Students should move beyond "they were weird" to consider cultural, economic, medical, or ingredient-quality reasons for different practices.

**5) Attempting translation—modern format:**

*Model Answer (Duck with Turnips):*

**INGREDIENTS:**
- 1 whole duck, cleaned
- Water, enough to cover
- 1 tsp salt (?)
- 2-3 sprigs fresh dill
- 1/4 cup vinegar
- 1 bunch leeks, chopped
- 2 Tbsp fresh coriander (cilantro?) leaves
- 2-3 medium turnips, chopped

**For sauce:**
- 1 tsp black pepper
- 1/2 tsp cumin
- 1/2 tsp coriander seed
- Laser root (UNAVAILABLE—substitute asafoetida? How much?)
- Fresh mint (how much?)
- Fresh rue (RARE—how much? Substitute?)
- 2 Tbsp vinegar
- 2 Tbsp honey
- 2 Tbsp *garum* (substitute fish sauce)
- 1/2 cup duck cooking liquid
- Extra black pepper for garnish

**INSTRUCTIONS:**
1. Place duck in large pot, cover with water, add salt, dill, and 1/4 cup vinegar
2. Bring to boil, reduce heat, parboil duck (HOW LONG? 20 min? 40 min?)
3. Add leeks and coriander; continue cooking (HOW LONG? Until duck internal temp reaches 165°F?)
4. Near end of cooking time, add turnips (WHEN EXACTLY? How do I know when duck is "nearly done"?); cook until tender
5. Meanwhile, make sauce: crush pepper, cumin, and coriander seed in mortar. Mix in laser/substitute, mint, and rue
6. Add vinegar, honey, *garum*, and 1/2 cup cooking liquid; mix well
7. Pour sauce over duck
8. Sprinkle with additional pepper; serve

**Problems encountered:** 
- **No measurements:** Original gives no quantities—"pepper" could mean 1/2 tsp or 2 Tbsp. Modern recipes need precision.
- **No times:** "Parboil" and "when nearly done" are vague—critical for food safety and doneness.
- **No temperatures:** Ancient cooking used visual/tactile cues; modern cooking uses thermometers and precise heat levels.
- **Substitute challenges:** Laser is extinct, rue is rare—hard to know what flavor profile we're approximating.
- **Preparation assumptions:** Recipe assumes knowledge of duck butchering, herb bundling technique, whether to leave duck whole or portion it.

*Alternative answer—Recipe C (Lentil Stew):* [Would show similar format with similar problems regarding measurements, timing, and extinct/rare ingredients]

**Grading notes:** Accept either recipe. Grading focuses on: (1) Proper modern format (ingredients listed separately with measurements, numbered instructions); (2) Identification of missing information—measurements, times, temperatures most important; (3) Recognition that ancient recipes assumed cultural knowledge modern cooks lack. Students demonstrating they understand gap between ancient and modern recipe-writing conventions should receive full credit even if specific measurements differ.

**6) Timothy's challenge—*laser* and *rue* substitutes:**

*Model Answer:*

For **laser** (silphium)—extinct plant family Apiaceae, described as pungent, garlicky, oniony, slightly bitter:
- **Substitute 1: Asafoetida** (resin from *Ferula* genus plants, related to silphium)—use tiny pinch (1/8 tsp or less), extremely pungent, provides similar sulfurous, onion-garlic notes. Available at Indian grocery stores as *hing*.
- **Substitute 2: Combination** of minced garlic (1 clove) + pinch of fenugreek + small amount grated horseradish—attempts to recreate complex pungent-bitter-aromatic profile from multiple ingredients.

For **rue** (*Ruta graveolens*)—bitter, slightly medicinal herb, can be toxic in large quantities:
- **Substitute 1: Combination** of fresh rosemary + tiny bit of lavender—provides herbal, slightly floral, mildly bitter notes approximating rue's character without toxicity risk.
- **Substitute 2: Fenugreek leaves** (*methi*)—bitter, slightly sweet, complex herbal flavor that provides some of rue's bitter complexity without being identical.

*Alternative acceptable substitutes:* Orange or lemon zest (for bitter aromatic quality), small amount of vermouth or fortified wine (for herbal complexity), celery leaf + thyme combination (family Apiaceae connection to laser).

**Grading notes:** Accept any reasonable substitutes with clear reasoning. Looking for: (1) Research/knowledge of what original ingredients tasted like; (2) Thoughtful flavor profile matching; (3) Consideration of availability and safety (rue's potential toxicity should be noted if student mentions it). Students don't need exotic substitute knowledge but should demonstrate problem-solving about how to approximate unfamiliar flavors using available ingredients.

**7) Class reflection—Roman vs. modern Italian continuity:**

*Model Answer:*

**What seems radically different:**
- **Sweet-savory fusion:** Modern Italian cooking generally separates sweet desserts (*dolci*) from savory mains (*primi/secondi*), rarely mixing honey or dates into meat sauces. Apicius freely combines these, creating flavor profiles unlike modern Italian food.
- ***Garum* disappearance:** Fish sauce was ubiquitous in Roman cooking, appearing virtually everywhere. Modern Italian food doesn't use fish sauce as baseline—except *colatura di alici* (anchovy sauce from Amalfi coast), which is minor specialty ingredient, not universal staple like Roman *garum*.
- **Heavy spicing:** Apicius uses intense spice combinations (pepper, cumin, coriander, etc.) in quantities foreign to modern Italian cooking, which tends toward simpler herb-forward seasoning (basil, oregano, rosemary, garlic, but not Indian-level spicing).
- **Exotic meats:** Dormice, ostrich, flamingo tongues featured in Roman elite dining; modern Italian cuisine doesn't include these.

**What seems continuous:**
- ***Agrodolce* tradition:** Sweet-sour sauces survive in modern Italian *agrodolce* (vinegar-sugar-based sauces), especially in Sicilian and Venetian cooking—though less extreme than Roman honey-vinegar-*garum* combinations, the flavor concept persists.
- **Basic ingredients—Mediterranean Triad:** Wheat bread, wine, and olive oil remain foundational to Italian cuisine just as they were for Romans. This continuity is real and significant.
- **Herbs:** Many Roman herbs (oregano, thyme, mint, parsley, rosemary) continue in Italian cooking, though combinations differ.
- **Pasta's absence then, presence now:** Interestingly, ancient Romans didn't eat pasta as centerpiece (though they had *laganum*, which might be pasta's ancestor)—modern Italian cuisine's signature element has different origin.
- **Regional specialization:** Roman empire had regional food specializations (Campanian wine, Spanish olive oil, Sicilian grain); modern Italy maintains strong regional food identities (Bolognese *ragù*, Neapolitan pizza, Venetian seafood, etc.)—this pattern continues.

**Overall assessment:** While there's some continuity (olive oil, wine, bread base; *agrodolce* tradition; regional variation), Roman elite cooking as represented in Apicius differs dramatically from modern Italian cuisine in flavor profiles, spice use, sweet-savory boundaries, and key ingredients (*garum*). Modern Italian cuisine was shaped more by medieval, Renaissance, and modern influences (tomatoes from Americas, pasta's development, French culinary influence, etc.) than by direct descent from Apicius. The statement "Italian food is based on ancient Roman cooking" is oversimplified—there's influence and some continuity, but also profound transformation across 1,500+ years.

**Grading notes:** Strong answers demonstrate nuanced understanding that some elements continued (basics like bread/wine/oil, general flavor ideas like *agrodolce*) while others changed radically (spice intensity, *garum*, sweet-savory mixing, specific ingredients). Students should avoid binary thinking ("completely same" or "totally different") and instead recognize partial continuity with major evolution. Credit for specific examples and thoughtful analysis.

---

## Activity 2: The *Triclinium* Seating Challenge - Roman Dinner Party Politics

**Objective:** Students apply understanding of Roman social hierarchy and dining customs by solving seating arrangement puzzle, then reflect on how food events communicate and reinforce social status.

**Instructions:**

You are organizing a *cena* (formal Roman dinner party) in your *triclinium* (dining room with three couches arranged in U-shape). The standard *triclinium* seats nine guests—three per couch. Seating positions carry significant social meaning, and placing guests incorrectly would be insulting disaster.

**COUCH POSITIONS** (in order of prestige, highest to lowest):
1. **Locus consularis** (consular position—middle couch, far right as host views it from entrance): MOST HONORED position, reserved for highest-status guest
2. **Lectus medius positions 2-3** (middle couch, center and left): High honor
3. **Lectus summus** (right couch when entering): Moderate honor
4. **Lectus imus** (left couch when entering): Lower status; host typically occupies middle position here to oversee service

**THE GUESTS** (you must seat all nine):

**1. Senator Quintus Fabius** - Powerful senator, military hero, owns vast estates across three provinces, age 55, your primary political patron who provides legal protection and recommends you for government positions. His support is essential to your career.

**2. Marcus Licinius** - Wealthy equestrian (business class, not quite senatorial rank), owns shipping fleet, has commercial relationship with you, age 40, genial personality, important business connection but lower social status than senators.

**3. Gaius Cornelius** - Your brother-in-law, senator of moderate influence, age 48, family connection requires courtesy but relationship is sometimes tense, middling political influence.

**4. Titus Aurelius** - Young senator, age 28, recently elected, rising star in politics, brilliant orator, potential future consul, comes from illustrious old patrician family. Honoring him now could pay dividends later when he's powerful.

**5. Lucius Septimius** - Minor municipal official, age 50, helped you with building permit recently, you owe small favor, friendly but limited influence outside local city politics.

**6. Your freedman/former slave Philippus** - Age 35, extremely capable, manages your household and business affairs, personally loyal, but legally and socially inferior to free-born citizens. Romans sometimes invited freedmen they trusted, but seating them above free-born guests would cause offense.

**7. Decimus Varro** - Scholar and philosopher, age 60, not politically powerful but intellectually prestigious, famous in literary circles, moderate wealth, sought-after dinner companion for brilliant conversation.

**8. Sextus Claudius** - Poet, age 32, witty, entertaining, writes flattering verses about prominent Romans (including potentially you if properly cultivated), moderately talented, not wealthy or politically powerful but adds sophisticated cultural atmosphere to dinners.

**9. Your nephew Gnaeus** - Age 22, just beginning career, no independent status yet but beloved family member whom you're helping launch into society. Needs to make good impressions on powerful men at dinner.

**YOUR TASK:**

**Part A:** Arrange all nine guests in the *triclinium*. List which couch (lectus medius, lectus summus, or lectus imus) each guest occupies and their specific position on that couch (positions 1-3, where position 1 is most honored on each couch). Remember: Locus consularis (most honored single spot) is position 1 of lectus medius.

**Part B:** Write 200-250 word explanation defending your seating choices. Address:
- Why you gave the most honored positions to those guests
- What risks or trade-offs your arrangement involves
- Whether any guests might be offended by their placement
- How you balanced political considerations, social status, family obligations, and personal relationships

**Part C:** Reflection (100-150 words): Compare this Roman seating politics to a modern social situation where seating/placement communicates status. Examples might include: wedding reception seating, corporate conference room positions, awards ceremony front-row seats, airplane cabin classes, restaurant table assignments. What's similar? What's different?

---

## Activity 2: Answer Key with Explanations

**Part A: Seating Arrangement**

*Model Answer:*

**LECTUS MEDIUS** (Middle couch—most prestigious):
- **Position 1 (Locus consularis—MOST HONORED):** Senator Quintus Fabius
- **Position 2:** Titus Aurelius (young rising senator)
- **Position 3:** Gaius Cornelius (brother-in-law senator)

**LECTUS SUMMUS** (Right couch—moderate honor):
- **Position 1:** Decimus Varro (philosopherscholar)
- **Position 2:** Marcus Licinius (wealthy equestrian businessman)
- **Position 3:** Gnaeus (nephew)

**LECTUS IMUS** (Left couch—lower status, contains host):
- **Position 1:** Sextus Claudius (poet)
- **Position 2:** HOST POSITION (you sit here to oversee service and converse with all guests)
- **Position 3:** Lucius Septimius (minor local official)

**PHILIPPUS (freedman):** NOT seated at *triclinium*—instead, invite him to join for *comissatio* (drinking party after formal dinner courses) or honor him separately at less formal meal. Seating him with senators would deeply offend them and damage your reputation.

*Note: Multiple defensible arrangements exist. What matters is thoughtful reasoning, not single "correct" answer.*

**Grading notes for Part A:** Accept variations if student provides solid justification. Key principles to check:
- Senator Fabius (primary patron) should be in most honored or very high position (usually locus consularis)
- Senators generally ranked above equestrians (business class)
- Family members placed appropriately considering their status
- Freedman either not seated or given low position (seating him above senators would be serious error)
- Rising star Titus should be honored fairly highly (investment in future relationship)
- Scholar/poet add cultural sophistication but lack political power—middling positions appropriate

**Part B: Explanation of Seating Choices**

*Model Answer (250 words):*

I placed Senator Fabius in the locus consularis (most honored position) because he is my primary patron—the foundation of my political career. Offending him would be catastrophic, while honoring him properly strengthens our patron-client bond. This is non-negotiable.

Titus Aurelius receives the second-most honored position despite his youth because he's rising political star from prestigious family. Honoring him now builds relationship that could benefit me enormously when he reaches higher office. Though older guests might feel slighted by young man's high placement, his family connections and political trajectory justify it as smart long-term investment.

Gaius Cornelius (brother-in-law) gets the third lectus medius position—family obligation requires honoring him, and his senatorial rank demands high placement despite our sometimes-tense relationship. Placing him lower would create family crisis.

Marcus Licinius is tricky. He's wealthy and important business partner but equestrian status (not senator) means protocol places him below senators. I've given him first position on lectus summus—highest rank for non-senators, showing respect for his wealth and our business relationship while maintaining status hierarchy.

Decimus Varro (scholar) shares the lectus summus because his intellectual prestige adds sophisticated tone to dinner, even without political power. His conversation will entertain Senator Fabius and others.

My nephew Gnaeus gets moderate position where he can observe powerful men and learn, without presuming status he hasn't earned yet. Lucius Septimius and Sextus Claudius fill remaining positions—appreciated but clearly lower status.

The freed man Philippus cannot sit with senators—Roman social rules prohibit it. I'll honor him separately.

*Main risks:* Marcus might resent being placed below three senators despite his wealth; Gaius might be annoyed his brother-in-law ranks below younger Titus; minor guests might feel undervalued.

*Alternative model answer emphasizing different choices:* [Could prioritize young Titus even higher, or balance business relationships differently, or handle brother-in-law otherwise—multiple reasonable arrangements exist]

**Grading rubric for Part B:**
- **Addresses patron relationship** and places Fabius very highly: 5 points
- **Demonstrates understanding of status hierarchy** (senators above equestrians, free-born above freed, etc.): 5 points
- **Considers political strategy** and future relationships (Titus as investment): 5 points
- **Acknowledges trade-offs and potential offenses** rather than claiming perfect solution: 4 points
- **Shows family obligation awareness** in placing nephew and brother-in-law: 3 points
- **Handles freedman appropriately** (recognizes he cannot sit above senators): 3 points
- **Clear writing within word count** (200-250): 2 points
- **Total:** 27 points

Accept alternative arrangements if reasoning is sound. More important that students demonstrate understanding of Roman status hierarchies, political calculations, and trade-offs than that they match specific "correct" answer.

**Part C: Modern Comparison Reflection**

*Model Answer (125 words):*

Wedding receptions provide closest modern parallel to Roman *triclinium* politics. The "head table" (like locus consularis) seats bride/groom and most honored guests (parents, wedding party), communicating their central importance. Other tables are hierarchically arranged—close family and honored guests near head table, distant relatives or less-significant acquaintances farther away. Couples agonize over seating charts exactly as Romans worried about *triclinium* arrangements, knowing offended aunts or slighted friends could create lasting conflicts. Similarities include: status communication through physical proximity to honored positions, careful balancing of family obligations versus friendship versus status considerations, and potential for serious offense from "wrong" placement.

Key difference: Modern weddings usually seat people at separate tables, distancing lower-status guests rather than having all ranks visible in single U-shaped arrangement where hierarchy is spatially obvious to everyone simultaneously. Roman *triclinium* made status performance more theatrical and immediate.

*Alternative model answers could discuss:*
- **Corporate meetings:** Head of conference table = most powerful, with positions radiating outward by status
- **Airplane classes:** First class/business/economy literally tiered system where money/status determines physical comfort and service quality
- **Awards shows:** Front-row seats for nominated celebrities, back rows for less famous attendees
- **Restaurant seating:** "Best tables" (window seats, quiet corners, visible-but-private spots) given to high-status or high-spending patrons

**Grading rubric for Part C:**
- **Identifies appropriate modern comparison** with clear status-communication element: 4 points
- **Explains similarities** to Roman triclinium seating politics: 3 points
- **Identifies meaningful difference(s)** between ancient and modern practice: 3 points
- **Clear writing within word count** (100-150 words): 2 points
- **Total:** 12 points

---

## Lesson 3 Quiz

**Instructions:** Answer all 15 questions. The quiz covers all five reading sections, videos, and activities. You need 68% (27/40 points) or better to pass. Multiple choice questions are worth 2 points each; short answer questions are worth 4 points each; geography questions are worth 3 points each.

---

**MULTIPLE CHOICE** (10 questions, 2 points each = 20 points total)

**1) Roman ships sailing to India exploited seasonal wind patterns. What were these winds called?**
a) Trade winds  
b) Westerlies  
c) Monsoons  
d) Sirocco

**2) Which Roman Red Sea port was a major departure point for ships sailing to India?**
a) Carthage  
b) Berenice  
c) Pompeii  
d) Ostia

**3) What was the Roman trade imbalance problem that worried writers like Pliny the Elder?**
a) Rome exported more than it imported, creating grain shortages  
b) Rome imported luxury goods but paid mostly in precious metals (gold/silver), draining wealth eastward  
c) Roman merchants were forbidden from trading beyond empire borders  
d) Pirates stole most trade goods before they reached Roman ports

**4) Which ingredient appeared in virtually every recipe in Apicius's cookbook?**
a) Pepper  
b) *Garum* (fish sauce)  
c) Honey  
d) Olive oil

**5) What fermentation process created *garum*'s distinctive umami flavor?**
a) Yeast converting sugars to alcohol  
b) Bacterial fermentation of vegetables in brine  
c) Enzymatic breakdown of fish proteins into amino acids (including glutamates) in salt  
d) Aging cheese cultures producing flavor compounds

**6) Which region was famous for premium *garum sociorum*, the most expensive fish sauce variety?**
a) Egypt  
b) Campania (southern Italy)  
c) Carthago Nova (Spain)  
d) Black Sea region

**7) In a Roman *triclinium*, which couch position was most honored?**
a) Lectus imus (lowest couch, left side)  
b) Locus consularis on lectus medius (middle couch, specific position)  
c) Any position on lectus summus (highest couch, right side)  
d) The position nearest the kitchen for freshest food

**8) What was the typical first course of a formal Roman *cena* called?**
a) *Prima mensa*  
b) *Gustatio* or *promulsis*  
c) *Secunda mensa*  
d) *Comissatio*

**9) Which social class received free or subsidized grain distributions through the *annona* system in Rome?**
a) All Roman citizens, including provincials throughout empire  
b) Qualified urban citizens (adult males with citizenship in Rome itself)  
c) Only senators and equestrians  
d) Slaves and freedmen exclusively

**10) What were *thermopolia* in Roman cities?**
a) Public bath complexes with hot springs  
b) Fast-food bars with counters holding hot prepared foods  
c) Temples dedicated to fire deities  
d) Gladiator training schools

---

**SHORT ANSWER** (3 questions, 4 points each = 12 points total)

**11) Explain the Roman "Mediterranean dominance" advantage for trade. How did controlling the entire Mediterranean coastline (rather than just portions) specifically enable commercial growth? Provide at least two concrete benefits.**

**12) Describe Roman elite flavor preferences as seen in Apicius's recipes. What combination of taste elements did they layer in single dishes? Why might this seem unusual to modern Western palates? (Provide specific examples.)**

**13) Compare diet and nutrition between elite Romans and urban working-class Romans (*plebs urbana*). Identify at least three major differences in what each group ate and explain why these differences existed.**

---

**GEOGRAPHY QUESTIONS** (2 questions, 3 points each = 6 points total)

**14) Name two specific Roman provinces (outside Italy) that were major exporters of grain feeding Rome's population. For each, briefly explain why that region could produce such large grain surpluses. (Think climate, river systems, or agricultural conditions.)**

**15) Identify the body of water Roman merchants crossed to reach India's western coast from Red Sea ports. Explain what made this dangerous journey economically worthwhile despite the risks and distance. (Be specific about what goods justified the voyage.)**

---

**BONUS QUESTION** (+2 points, optional)

**B1) The extinct plant *laser* (silphium) was so valuable in Roman cooking and medicine that it appeared on Cyrenaic coins and sources claim it was harvested to extinction. Based on ancient descriptions (garlicky, pungent, slightly bitter, resin extracted from plant family Apiaceae), culinary historians believe which modern plant/spice is most closely related?**
a) Black pepper  
b) Asafoetida (*hing*)  
c) Sesame  
d) Vanilla

## Lesson 3 Quiz - Answer Key with Explanations

**1) C - Monsoons**

*Explanation:* Monsoon winds in the Indian Ocean blow eastward toward India during summer months, then reverse and blow westward toward Arabia/Africa during winter. Roman merchants sailing from Red Sea ports like Berenice could ride summer monsoons across open ocean to India, then return months later when winter monsoons reversed direction. This direct route, systematized around 1st century BCE after Greek navigator Hippalus documented the patterns, revolutionized Indian trade by replacing slower, more dangerous coastal routes through Persian Gulf controlled by middlemen. Monsoons made India economically accessible.

*Why other answers are wrong:* Trade winds are tropical Atlantic/Pacific patterns; westerlies are mid-latitude Northern Hemisphere winds; Sirocco is Mediterranean hot south wind—none specifically enable Indian Ocean crossing like monsoons.

**2) B - Berenice**

*Explanation:* Berenice was purpose-built Ptolemaic (later Roman) port on Egypt's Red Sea coast specifically for Indian Ocean trade. Located far south along Red Sea, Berenice minimized Mediterranean-to-Indian-Ocean overland distance—goods arrived by ship from India, were unloaded, transported overland across Eastern Desert to Nile, then shipped down Nile to Alexandria and Mediterranean. Myos Hormos was another major Red Sea port. Archaeological excavations at Berenice have found Indian pottery, pepper, textiles, and goods from Southeast Asia, confirming its role in spice trade. These Red Sea ports were absolutely critical to Roman-Indian commerce.

*Why other answers are wrong:* Carthage was North African port on Mediterranean coast (not Red Sea); Pompeii was inland Italian city (not port); Ostia was Rome's Mediterranean port (never involved in Indian trade directly).

**3) B - Rome imported luxury goods but paid mostly in precious metals, draining wealth eastward**

*Explanation:* Ancient sources, especially Pliny the Elder's *Natural History*, complained that Roman gold and silver flowed eastward to India, China, and Arabia to purchase luxuries (especially silk, pepper, other spices, gemstones) while Romans exported relatively little of equal value—creating persistent trade deficit. Pliny estimated 50-100 million *sesterces* annually left Rome for Eastern goods, calling it drainage for "feminine luxuries" and unnecessary ornaments. Rome paid primarily in precious metals (gold/silver coins, bullion) obtained from Spanish mines and conquest plunder. Some historians speculate this long-term precious metal drainage contributed to Later Empire economic problems, though others debate this. The imbalance was economically significant and culturally controversial even in ancient times.

*Why other answers are wrong:* Rome imported more than it exported (not opposite); there were no legal prohibitions on external trade (encouraged, in fact); piracy was suppressed during Pax Romana's height.

**4) B - *Garum* (fish sauce)**

*Explanation:* *Garum* (also called *liquamen*) appears in nearly every recipe in Apicius's *De Re Coquinaria*—sauces, meat dishes, fish, vegetables, even some recipes for sick people. Romans used *garum* where modern cooks use salt, and it provided both salinity and savory umami depth. Virtually no Roman recipe lacks *garum*/liquamen—it was cooking's foundational flavoring. Other ingredients like pepper, honey, and olive oil appear frequently but not quite as universally as *garum*. Understanding *garum*'s ubiquity is essential to understanding Roman cooking—remove it and dishes wouldn't taste "Roman."

*Why other answers are wrong:* Pepper was expensive and widely used but not literally in every recipe; honey and olive oil were very common but not absolutely universal like *garum*.

**5) C - Enzymatic breakdown of fish proteins into amino acids (including glutamates) in salt**

*Explanation:* *Garum* was made by salting small fish (or fish innards like guts which contain digestive enzymes), mixing with herbs, and fermenting in sun for 2-3 months. Fish gut enzymes broke down proteins (*proteolysis*) into component amino acids, including glutamic acid—the compound that creates umami (savory) taste. High salt concentration prevented pathogenic spoilage bacteria while allowing fermentation. The result was stable, intensely savory liquid packed with flavor compounds and amino acids. Modern food science explains this as controlled enzymatic fermentation producing glutamates (umami) and other flavor molecules. Other fermented fish products (Southeast Asian fish sauces, Japanese *ishi shiokara*) use similar processes producing similar umami-rich results.

*Why other answers are wrong:* Yeast-sugar-alcohol is wine/beer fermentation; vegetable-brine is sauerkraut/kimchi-style lacto-fermentation; cheese aging involves bacteria/fungi but different process from fish enzyme fermentation.

**6) C - Carthago Nova (Spain)**

*Explanation:* Carthago Nova (modern Cartagena) on Spain's southeastern coast was renowned for *garum sociorum*—premium fish sauce commanding luxury prices rivaling expensive wines. Pliny the Elder and other sources specifically mention Spanish *garum*, particularly from Carthago Nova and Atlantic coast, as highest quality. Spain's Atlantic and Mediterranean coastlines had numerous *garum* factories (*cetariae*) with archaeological remains showing large fermentation vats, processing facilities, and massive fish bone deposits. Spain dominated commercial *garum* production empire-wide, exporting in standardized amphorae. North Africa and Black Sea regions also produced respected *garum*, but Spanish product, especially from Carthago Nova, was most prestigious.

*Why other answers are wrong:* Egypt was grain supplier, not famous for *garum*; Campania/Pompeii had *garum* production but smaller-scale than Spanish; Black Sea produced specialty *garum* but not as commercially dominant as Spain.

**7) B - Locus consularis on lectus medius (middle couch, specific position)**

*Explanation:* Roman *triclinium* had three couches arranged in U-shape: lectus medius (middle), lectus summus (right/high), lectus imus (left/low). The single most honored position was *locus consularis* ("consular position")—specific spot on lectus medius traditionally reserved for guest of honor, highest-status attendee, or patron. Its name references Roman consul (highest elected office)—position fit for consul. Being placed there was supreme compliment; placing wrong person there would be major gaffe. Seating hierarchies were rigid and meaningful—every position communicated status to all attendees. Hosts agonized over seating arrangements because they were public status declarations.

*Why other answers are wrong:* Lectus imus was lowest-status couch (host often sat there to oversee service); lectus summus was moderately prestigious but not top; proximity to kitchen was never status consideration (service came from elsewhere).

**8) B - *Gustatio* or *promulsis***

*Explanation:* Formal *cenae* followed three-part structure: (1) *Gustatio* or *promulsis* (appetizers)—eggs, salads, shellfish, light dishes stimulating appetite, accompanied by *mulsum* (honey-wine); (2) *Prima mensa* (main course)—multiple meat, fish, and vegetable dishes served sequentially; (3) *Secunda mensa* (dessert/"second table")—fruit, nuts, cakes, cheese. The phrase "ab ovo usque ad mala" (from egg to apples) meant "soup-to-nuts"/"complete meal" because eggs traditionally opened dinners and fruit finished them. Understanding course structure is essential to grasping how elaborate elite *cenae* were—multi-hour theatrical performances with carefully paced dishes.

*Why other answers are wrong:* *Prima mensa* was main course (second); *secunda mensa* was dessert (third); *comissatio* was post-dinner drinking party/entertainment phase.

**9) B - Qualified urban citizens (adult males with citizenship in Rome itself)**

*Explanation:* The *annona* was Rome's grain distribution system providing free (later subsidized) grain to qualified citizens in city of Rome. Initially perhaps 40,000 qualified; Julius Caesar cut to 150,000; Augustus eventually around 200,000. Requirements: adult male, Roman citizen, resident of Rome proper (not provincial cities). This was political stability tool—"bread and circuses" keeping urban populace fed and content, preventing riots. Grain came from Sicily, North Africa, especially Egypt after Roman conquest (30 BCE). Eventually distribution expanded to baked bread rather than raw grain. The *annona* wasn't welfare for all, but privilege of urban citizenship designed to keep potentially dangerous unemployed mob politically supportive.

*Why other answers are wrong:* Provincials didn't qualify (city of Rome only); not limited to elite (targeted urban poor/working-class); slaves and freedmen generally didn't qualify (citizen requirement).

**10) B - Fast-food bars with counters holding hot prepared foods**

*Explanation:* *Thermopolia* (singular *thermopolium*) were Roman fast-food establishments featuring street-facing counters with built-in ceramic jars (*dolia*) holding hot prepared foods: stews, porridges, chickpea paste, lentils, sausages, sometimes wine. Working-class urban Romans who lacked cooking facilities in apartments bought meals from *thermopolia* rather than cooking at home. Pompeii's archaeological remains preserve numerous *thermopolia* with intact counters, dolia, wall paintings advertising offerings, and occasional remains of food (fish bones, olive pits proving what was served). These were ubiquitous in Roman cities—ancient equivalent of grab-and-go lunch spots, street vendor food, or modern fast-food restaurants. Critical for urban working-class diet.

*Why other answers are wrong:* Public baths were *thermae* (different word); temples to fire deities were shrines, not commercial establishments; gladiator schools were *lud* (no food connection).

---

**11) Roman Mediterranean dominance advantages:**

*Model Answer (strong response, 4/4 points):*

Controlling the entire Mediterranean coastline (rather than just portions) provided Romans several critical commercial advantages. **First, elimination of tariffs and trade barriers**—goods moved freely between provinces without customs inspections, import taxes, or protectionist policies that typically slow inter-polity trade. A merchant could ship olive oil from Spain to Egypt without paying fees at multiple ports or dealing with competing regulations—Mediterranean became internal commercial zone. **Second, unified legal and currency systems**—Roman commercial law applied empire-wide, enabling contract enforcement; Roman coinage circulated everywhere, facilitating transactions without money-changing. **Third, piracy suppression**—Roman naval power eliminated pirates who plagued earlier Mediterranean shipping, making maritime trade far safer and reducing insurance costs/lost cargoes. **Fourth, harbor infrastructure investment**—Rome built ports, lighthouses, breakwaters across Mediterranean, improving ship safety and efficiency. Together, these advantages made Mediterranean trade faster, cheaper, safer, and more profitable than fragmented political control ever allowed—"Mare Nostrum" ("Our Sea") was truly integrated economic zone.

*Grading notes:* Full credit (4 points) requires at least two concrete, well-explained benefits with clear connection to Mediterranean unification. Accept any of: tariff/barrier elimination, legal unification, currency standardization, piracy suppression, infrastructure investment, political stability. Weaker answers (2-3 points) might list benefits without explanation or provide vague statements ("trade was easier") without concrete mechanisms.

---

**12) Roman elite flavor preferences:**

*Model Answer (strong response, 4/4 points):*

Roman elite cuisine, exemplified in Apicius's recipes, layered multiple taste elements in single dishes: **sweet** (honey, dates, raisined wine/*passum*), **sour** (vinegar, wine), **salty-savory** (*garum* fish sauce), and **spicy/aromatic** (pepper, cumin, coriander, multiple herbs like mint and rue). This *agrodolce* (sweet-sour) complexity with added fish sauce and intense spicing creates flavor profiles unusual to modern Western palates. For example, Apicius's duck with turnips combines honey (sweet), vinegar (sour), *garum* (salty-umami), and pepper-cumin-coriander-mint-rue (complex spicing) in single sauce—layers that modern cooking usually separates. Similarly, "Sweet Wine Cakes" (dessert) are honey-soaked then sprinkled with black pepper—combining sweetness with sharp spice modern desserts avoid. Contemporary Western cuisine tends toward clearer boundaries: savory mains rarely contain honey; desserts avoid fish sauce and strongly avoid pepper. Romans valued complexity and intensity, preferring bold multi-layered tastes to subtle single-note flavors. This reflects cultural preference, not objective superiority—many global cuisines (Thai, Vietnamese, Persian) similarly layer sweet-sour-salty-spicy successfully.

*Grading notes:* Full credit (4 points) requires: (1) identification of specific taste combinations (sweet-sour-salty-spicy), (2) concrete Apicius examples, (3) explanation of why unusual to modern Western tastes. Partial credit (2-3 points) for incomplete explanations or missing examples.

---

**13) Elite versus urban working-class diet comparison:**

*Model Answer (strong response, 4/4 points):*

Elite and urban working-class Roman diets differed dramatically in three major ways:

**Ingredient variety and exotic imports:** Elite consumed imported luxuries—Indian pepper, silk-wrapped game, African ostrich, Spanish premium *garum*, Persian dates, multi-course meals with theatrical presentations. Urban *plebs* ate monotonous bread/porridge from grain dole, beans, cheap local vegetables, occasional small fish, lowest-grade *allec* (cheap fish sauce residue), thin wine or vinegar-water (*posca*). Elite could afford and access three-continent trade networks; poor ate whatever cost least and filled stomachs.

**Meat consumption:** Elite featured meat at nearly every *cena*—pork, lamb, venison, wild boar, multiple poultry varieties, elaborate seafood. Urban workers rarely ate meat except occasional small fish or at public festivals providing free distributions—meat was expensive luxury beyond daily reach. This protein difference affected nutrition and health significantly.

**Preparation and dining setting:** Elite employed teams of skilled slave cooks executing complex multi-stage Apicius recipes, served aboard silver platters in elegantــــ *triclinia* during multi-hour theatrical banquets. Urban workers bought pre-cooked simple stews from *thermopolia* counters (lacking home cooking facilities), eating quickly between work shifts, no elaborate service or dining rooms. One group treated eating as art form and status theater; other treated it as biological necessity.

These differences existed because: wealth inequality (elite controlled vast fortunes from land ownership/trade; workers earned minimal wages), political privilege (elite controlled grain distribution determining how much was sold versus given free), trade network

s serving elite tastes (merchants imported what rich Romans wanted, not what poor needed), and slavery enabling elite to command unpaid culinary labor while workers couldn't afford even basic cooking fuel.

*Grading notes:* Full credit (4 points) requires three major, well-explained differences plus some discussion of reasons/causes. Partial credit for fewer differences or incomplete explanations. Accept variations: elite/poor differences in meal elaborateness, social dining versus solitary eating, nutritional adequacy, food security, etc.

---

**14) Grain-exporting provinces:**

*Model Answer (strong response, 3/3 points):*

**Egypt (specifically the Nile Delta):** After Roman conquest (30 BCE), Egypt became empire's primary grain supplier, eventually providing majority of Rome city's grain needs through the *annona* system. Egypt could produce massive grain surpluses because the Nile's annual flooding deposited nutrient-rich silt renewing soil fertility naturally without fallowing, enabling intensive continuous cultivation. Flat delta and valley geography plus organized irrigation works maximized productive acreage. Egypt had been Mediterranean grain exporter for centuries (feeding Classical Athens earlier); Romans systematically exploited this agricultural bounty, treating Egypt as personal imperial possession with grain shipped directly to Rome.

**North Africa (especially Tunisia/eastern Algeria region, Roman provinces of Africa Proconsularis and Numidia):** North African coastal regions produced substantial grain surpluses due to relatively reliable rainfall (for Mediterranean climate), fertile plains, and Roman investment in agricultural infrastructure after defeating Carthage. Grain shipped relatively short distance across Mediterranean to Rome. North Africa remains significant grain producer today (though desertification has changed some areas).

*Alternative acceptable answers:* Sicily (first Roman province, major grain supplier during Republic period before Egypt's conquest); Sardinia (smaller but significant supplier); parts of Spain.

*Grading notes:* Full credit (3 points) for two specific provinces/regions with reasonable geographic/agricultural explanations. Accept Egypt + North Africa, Sicily, Sardinia, or Spain if plausible explanations provided. Partial credit (1.5 points) for naming regions without explanation or vague answers.

---

**15) Indian Ocean crossing:**

*Model Answer (strong response, 3/3 points):*

Roman merchants crossed the **Indian Ocean** (sometimes called Erythraean Sea in ancient sources) from Red Sea ports to reach India's western coast (Malabar Coast, especially Kerala). This dangerous multi-week open-ocean voyage crossing thousands of miles through unpredictable seas, far from safe harbor, with navigational challenges and monsoon timing requirements, was economically worthwhile because imported goods commanded extraordinary prices in Rome justifying risks. Specifically: **Black pepper** from Kerala's Malabar Coast was Rome's most desired spice, literally worth its weight in silver—small cargo volume with huge value. **Other valuable spices** (cinnamon, ginger, cardamom) and **aromatic substances** commanded premium prices. **Precious gemstones** (diamonds, sapphires, pearls) and **fine cotton textiles** were lightweight/high-value—perfect for long-distance trade. **Exotic luxuries** (ivory, certain medicines, specialty products) served insatiable elite demand. The profit margins on successful voyages were enormous—enough to offset voyage costs, shipwreck risks (insurance via spreading investment across multiple ships), and time investment. One successful pepper cargo could make merchant's fortune.

*Grading notes:* Full credit (3 points) for correctly identifying Indian Ocean AND explaining specific valuable goods (especially pepper) justifying voyage. Partial credit (1.5-2 points) for correct ocean without good explanation or vague goods ("spices, luxury stuff").

---

**BONUS B1) B - Asafoetida (*hing*)**

*Explanation:* Culinary historians and botanists believe asafoetida—pungent resin extracted from *Ferula* genus plants (family Apiaceae) used in Indian/Persian cuisine—is closely related to extinct Roman *laser* (silphium). Ancient descriptions of *laser* match asafoetida's characteristics: intensely pungent "garlicky-oniony" aroma, slightly bitter taste, resin extracted from large umbell iferous plants, same plant family. Silphium grew specifically in Cyrenaica (Libya) and couldn't be successfully cultivated elsewhere—likely overharvested to extinction by 1st century CE due to enormous demand. Romans prized it for culinary and medicinal uses, considering it near-miraculous substance. Image on Cyrenaic coins shows silphium plant. Modern recipes recreating Apicius dishes substitute asafoetida for *laser* with reasonable success—tiny quantities provide similar pungent flavor. Available at Indian grocery stores as *hing* powder.

---

**Overall Passing Score:** 27/40 (68%) or better

---

*End of Lesson 3: Empire of Flavor - Roman Trade and Gastronomy*
